{"uri": "2024-07-416961621", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "05:19:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T05:19:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T04:02:59Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9882352948188782, "url": "https://www.register-herald.com/news/nation_world/biden-to-meet-wednesday-with-top-union-leaders-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_d9d46362-1199-5925-bee5-1957ce2824b4.html", "title": "Biden to meet Wednesday with top union leaders as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat is set to meet Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.", {"uri": "2024-07-417298527", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "10:43:20", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T10:43:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T10:19:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9882352948188782, "url": "https://www.register-herald.com/news/nation_world/biden-will-meet-wednesday-with-top-union-leaders-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_d9d46362-1199-5925-bee5-1957ce2824b4.html", "title": "Biden will meet Wednesday with top union leaders as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "FILE - President Joe Biden speaks as AFL-CIO President Liz Shuler, right, listens, Dec. 8, 2022, in Washington. Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age. Biden is set to meet Wednesday, July 10, 2024, with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions. \"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler said.\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat is set to meet Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. {"uri": "8218863481", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "14:13:32", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T14:13:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T14:13:14Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9882352948188782, "url": "https://www.pbs.org/newshour/politics/biden-to-meet-with-union-leaders-in-effort-to-reassure-anxious-democrats", "title": "Biden to meet with union leaders in effort to reassure anxious Democrats", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat is set to meet Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions.\n\nWATCH: Democrats navigate divisions around supporting Biden or calling for him to step aside\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nREAD MORE: House Democrats meet as Biden's support on Capitol Hill hangs in the balance\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16 percent of voters came from union households and 56 percent of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.", {"uri": "2024-07-417327905", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "11:07:21", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T11:07:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T10:19:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9803921580314636, "url": "https://www.kulr8.com/news/national/biden-will-meet-wednesday-with-top-union-leaders-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_3ab46388-c54d-59c1-8fb2-4b690b001721.html", "title": "Biden will meet Wednesday with top union leaders as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat is set to meet Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nMore from this section\n\nVice Capital Markets Launches Integration with Fannie Mae Mission Score API\n\n[Not Banned] Failed to Detect Location 12 Error Fixed by iToolab AnyGo Pok\u00e9mon {"uri": "8219007854", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "15:47:22", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T15:47:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T15:47:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9686274528503418, "url": "https://www.bostonherald.com/2024/07/10/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions. He told the crowd that even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions, as he once again articulated his vision for an economy built \"from the bottom up and middle out.\"\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well guess what? I am.\"\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nBiden thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler says Biden has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.", He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his {"uri": "2024-07-418120206", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "03:50:34", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T03:50:34Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T23:33:06Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.95686274766922, "url": "https://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2024/jul/10/biden-looks-to-afl-cio-union-leaders-for-support-a/", "title": "'My domestic NATO': Biden looks to union leaders for support amid growing calls to step aside", "body": "President Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO - not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back - we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out - saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.", {"uri": "8219590598", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "01:21:37", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T01:21:37Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T01:20:39Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.95686274766922, "url": "https://journalnow.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_6589606e-2063-5482-b114-72e934c65d67.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, in an effort to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters. He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nFILE - President Joe Biden addresses the AFL-CIO convention, June 14, 2022, in Philadelphia. Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age. Biden is set to meet July 10, 2024, with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions. (AP Photo/Susan Walsh)\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out - saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers\u00b4 backs - and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he\u00b4s on top of the details. He has my support and we\u00b4re ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump\u00b4s time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees\u00b4 tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it\u00b4s different because I\u00b4ve employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. {"uri": "8219714392", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "03:50:37", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T03:50:37Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T03:50:10Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9450980424880981, "url": "https://newsadvance.com/partners/ap/national/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_3ad409d3-5da6-5bc9-8094-89278b9e667f.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nAlso Read | CNN Layoffs: US-Based Cable News Network Cutting About 100 Jobs, Plans to Debut Digital Subscriptions Before Year's End.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory. {"uri": "2024-07-416915938", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "04:14:45", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T04:14:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T00:02:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://www.usnews.com/news/us/articles/2024-07-10/biden-to-meet-wednesday-with-top-union-leaders-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats", "title": "Biden to Meet Wednesday With Top Union Leaders as He Seeks to Reassure Worried Democrats", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- Facing pressure from within his own party to abandon his reelection campaign, President Joe Biden is relying on labor unions to help make the case that his record in office matters more than his age.\n\nThe 81-year-old Democrat is set to meet Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nA person who has been involved in past executive council meetings described them as largely informal and unscripted, a sign that Biden will not be able to rely on a prepared text as he seeks to solidify support among a group of union leaders who are both loyal to his administration and pragmatic. The person insisted on anonymity to preview the private meeting.\n\nA White House official, insisting on anonymity to preview the meeting, said that Biden intends to thank the union leaders for their support and outline his plans for the future. Biden is close to many union leaders who will be in the room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"President Biden and Vice President Harris have always had workers' backs -- and we will have theirs,\" Shuler, the AFL-CIO president, said after the debate.\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
