He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... 3 dead in plane crash near McDowell, Rutherford county line Plane crashes in McDowell; emergency crews ask people to avoid Brackett Town Road Marion man meets McDowell paramedic who saved his life 24 years ago Toddler killed by truck at Steel Creek campground north of Morganton 3 from South Carolina die in Nebo plane crash; national safety board investigating Golden Hour Charcuterie opens in Marion's Depot District Body found Sunday was Marion man; cause of death pending toxicology report Nebo woman charged with break-in McDowell Little League crowns two champions; league to host a baseball state tournament Burkemont Mountain in Morganton ablaze with 30-acre wildfire Marion NC man's charges upgraded to felony after assault on 79-year-old When are the 4th of July fireworks, parades in McDowell County? McDowell calendar: Independence Day parades set for today Frisbee's Galaxy Supermarket in Old Fort now a Piggly Wiggly; still family owned Marion's Spillway Bridge & Co. set to reopen under new owners\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8219719985", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "03:57:20", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T03:57:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T03:56:52Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8666666746139526, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/partners/ap/national/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_35cc979d-d87e-57d4-94fa-274f7955dddc.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... Man found dead behind the wheel of vehicle on Veterans Parkway, police say 8-year-old boy dies after Wednesday night crash on I-74 Body pulled from Illinois River identified as Bloomington teen Bloomington man jailed on weapons, drug charges after traffic stop Bloomington woman faces DUI in crash that killed her 8-year-old son Flick Fact: How many State Farm employees are college educated? LaSalle County man dies in illegal fireworks incident Pub II marking 50th anniversary with party in uptown Normal Flick Fact: The $800M ex-farm girl from Stanford and Olympia High? Man found unresponsive in Bloomington had suffered medical event, police said McLean County man detained on cocaine sales charges Behind the Pritzker administration's quest to signal Illinois is 'open for business' Revolution Dispensary to host grand opening Friday in Normal Funerals pending for July 4 Opening statements begin in trial of man accused of killing teen at NYE party in Normal\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Scholten earns win in last-minute start for Sioux City Explorers Ex-Plymouth County deputy to plead guilty of stealing drugs Issues continue to plague Woodbury County jail project, sheriff shares concerns Body of missing 17-year-old who jumped into Platte River to save girl found, authorities say Saturday in the Park 2024 kicks-off with high spirits\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Scholten earns win in last-minute start for Sioux City Explorers Ex-Plymouth County deputy to plead guilty of stealing drugs Issues continue to plague Woodbury County jail project, sheriff shares concerns Body of missing 17-year-old who jumped into Platte River to save girl found, authorities say Saturday in the Park 2024 kicks-off with high spirits\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8219589139", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "01:19:43", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T01:19:43Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T01:18:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://heraldcourier.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_52619e10-2e17-5b77-aa56-526b04465327.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, in an effort to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters. He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... TVA announces changes at South Holston, Boone properties LITTLE LEAGUE: Good day for local teams in state softball tournament McNew living her dream as a large truck mechanic 'Vampire' series gives Sam Reid plenty to bite into PREP FOOTBALL: Shane Wicks gets first head-coaching opportunity at Honaker Simmons to expand manufacturing into Russell County AUTO RACING: Rural Retreat native Will Hostetler's a hometown hero at Wythe Raceway COLLEGE BASEBALL: Chase Hungate (Abingdon) headed to Mississippi State, relishes chance to compete in SEC LITTLE LEAGUE: State tournaments set across region and state PREP FOOTBALL: The VHSL master schedule was released on Thursday. Here are some games with intriguing storylines Marion drive in entrance design sparks controversy, extra look by officials COLLEGE FOOTBALL: You can find Brad Robbins (Powell Valley) coaching at UConn now Parades, music, fireworks on tap for Independence Day LITTLE LEAGUE: Monday's State Tournament Results PREP FOOTBALL: Chris Long says Heath Miller is right man for job at St. Anne's-Belfield\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... Man found dead behind the wheel of vehicle on Veterans Parkway, police say 8-year-old boy dies after Wednesday night crash on I-74 Body pulled from Illinois River identified as Bloomington teen Bloomington woman faces DUI in crash that killed her 8-year-old son Flick Fact: How many State Farm employees are college educated? Bloomington man jailed on weapons, drug charges after traffic stop LaSalle County man dies in illegal fireworks incident Pub II marking 50th anniversary with party in uptown Normal Flick Fact: The $800M ex-farm girl from Stanford and Olympia High? Man found unresponsive in Bloomington had suffered medical event, police said McLean County man detained on cocaine sales charges Behind the Pritzker administration's quest to signal Illinois is 'open for business' Funerals pending for July 4 Opening statements begin in trial of man accused of killing teen at NYE party in Normal Revolution Dispensary to host grand opening Friday in Normal\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "2024-07-418120292", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "03:49:46", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T03:49:46Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T03:33:06Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8470588326454163, "url": "https://qctimes.com/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_faa3d5c4-6467-58d4-8b8e-e039744e106a.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nAn early look at Davenport's plans for Main Street Landing Chicago company buys apartment buildings in Davenport, Moline Quad-Cities business openings, closings: Shelly's Sweet Sensations, Crawford Brew Works and more Agriculture expert: Deere prepares for future losses due to declining ag economy Moline woman gave report of missing mail to postal worker accused of stealing mail Driver of vehicle in LeClaire crash identified John Deere closes on land for 1.2 million-square-foot warehouse in Indiana 7 Quad Cities restaurants that opened in June Police identify woman killed by crash Monday afternoon northwest of Davenport Matt Moody managed the Village Theater and touched many lives NEW DETAILS: John Deere announces another 345 layoffs at Waterloo facilities Review: Lainey Wilson sings in the rain Sunday at the John Deere Classic Review: Counting Crows at the John Deere Classic 'Concerts on the Course' Brazilian bomber Bolkema excelling Davenport North Sarah Goodwin says her husband's life was 'swirling out of control' before he was killed\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8219717240", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-11", "time": "03:54:04", "dateTime": "2024-07-11T03:54:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-11T03:53:31Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.843137264251709, "url": "https://heraldcourier.com/partners/ap/national/biden-looks-to-union-leaders-for-support-as-he-seeks-to-reassure-worried-democrats/article_52619e10-2e17-5b77-aa56-526b04465327.html", "title": "Biden looks to union leaders for support as he seeks to reassure worried Democrats", "body": "By JOSH BOAK - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- President Joe Biden met Wednesday with the executive council of the AFL-CIO, America's largest federation of trade unions, to shore up support from a critical constituency as he beats back continued calls to step aside in the 2024 campaign.\n\n\"I think of you as my domestic NATO -- not a joke,\" the 81-year-old Democrat told the crowd gathered at headquarters.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... TVA announces changes at South Holston, Boone properties LITTLE LEAGUE: Good day for local teams in state softball tournament McNew living her dream as a large truck mechanic 'Vampire' series gives Sam Reid plenty to bite into PREP FOOTBALL: Shane Wicks gets first head-coaching opportunity at Honaker Simmons to expand manufacturing into Russell County AUTO RACING: Rural Retreat native Will Hostetler's a hometown hero at Wythe Raceway COLLEGE BASEBALL: Chase Hungate (Abingdon) headed to Mississippi State, relishes chance to compete in SEC LITTLE LEAGUE: State tournaments set across region and state PREP FOOTBALL: The VHSL master schedule was released on Thursday. Here are some games with intriguing storylines Marion drive in entrance design sparks controversy, extra look by officials COLLEGE FOOTBALL: You can find Brad Robbins (Powell Valley) coaching at UConn now Parades, music, fireworks on tap for Independence Day LITTLE LEAGUE: Monday's State Tournament Results PREP FOOTBALL: Chris Long says Heath Miller is right man for job at St. Anne's-Belfield\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well, guess what? I am.\"\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat-out told the president that he had the union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nA person familiar with the meeting said it reflected some of the divisions among Democrats about Biden. One union leader pushed Biden to do more to show he's a fighter, while the head of the North America's Building Trades Unions said the Democrats calling for Biden to step down didn't know how to fight tough. The person insisted on anonymity to discuss the closed-door gathering.\n\nUnited Auto Workers President Shawn Fain, who Biden had actively courted during last year's strikes, was not recognized to speak while Biden was in the room. But he later pushed Biden's team to explain their plans for beating Trump.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration, despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. The AFL-CIO said Thursday that Biden's \"fighting spirit\" was on full display in the back and forth with union leaders.\n\nStill, some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\nThe AFL-CIO said it unanimously voted on Thursday to \"reaffirm\" its backing of \"President Joe Biden and Vice President Kamala Harris, the leadership of the most pro-union administration in our lifetimes.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting was a test of whether Biden's emphasis on policy and loyalty to Democratic constituencies such as unions can overcome doubts about his candidacy.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. He extolled the power of unions and told them of his longstanding support for them, telling the crowd even Wall Street was acknowledging the power of unions.\n\nHours later in the Oval Office, when a reporter asked him about an opinion piece written by George Clooney in which the actor implored Biden to leave the 2024 race, the president responded with \"AFL-CIO!\" paused, pumped his fists and added, \"Go, go, go.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... Sattui Winery sued by former employee alleging wage abuse St. Helena postpones Fourth of July fireworks show due to hot, dry conditions\n\nThe AFL-CIO said the president has been booked to attend the meeting for more than a year, but his participation now involves much higher scrutiny after his weak debate performance against Donald Trump raised fears about his ability to compete in November's election. His sit-down with union officials also overlaps with the NATO summit in Washington, where Biden is navigating geopolitics with other world leaders.\n\nBiden spoke with a handheld mic and no teleprompter in sight. He thanked the union leaders for their support, and outlined his plans for the future seeking to cast aside doubt over his reelection campaign. Biden is close to many union leaders who were in room, and considers AFL-CIO president Liz Shuler to be a personal friend.\n\nIn announcing Biden, Shuler told the room he has supported jobs, manufacturing and created good union jobs in clean energy. She flat out told the president that he had union's support.\n\n\"You've always had our back -- we have your back,\" Shuler said to the president.\n\nThe council is composed of more than 50 officials from the unions that compose the AFL-CIO, with the group representing 12.5 million union members.\n\nSo far, the unions are sticking with the Biden administration despite widespread fears that his age handicaps his candidacy after his shaky performance in the June 27 debate. But some statements of support are also worded diplomatically to suggest a degree of flexibility in case Biden chooses to drop out -- saying they back the Biden-Harris administration and not just Biden personally.\n\n\"I said I'm going to be the most pro-union president in American history,\" Biden told the cheering crowd. \"Well. guess what? I am.\"\n\nAfter Biden was interviewed by ABC News last week in the aftermath of his poor debate, Randi Weingarten, president of the American Federation of Teachers, posted on X: \"Biden is an incredible President and tonight we saw that he's on top of the details. He has my support and we're ready to keep working for Biden-Harris to win in November.\"\n\nSome union leaders have been more targeted in their support for Biden and his continued candidacy.\n\nUnited Steelworkers International President David McCall said before Wednesday's meeting that his union \"proudly supports\" Biden, saying that his \"record of delivering for working people stands for itself.\"\n\nKenneth Cooper, president of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, was also firmly behind Biden, saying that his union members \"couldn't ask for a stronger advocate.\"\n\nWednesday's meeting has become a test of the union movement's strategy to emphasize Biden's policy agenda as a way to overcome doubts about his candidacy. Biden routinely holds his events at union halls. He has conversed regularly with several of the union leaders at Wednesday's meeting, knowing that the group is a key link at the local level to the voters that he says make him the Democrats' best candidate in November.\n\nOver the course of his term, Biden has tied his administration to the idea that labor unions built the middle class. He visited a picket line during the auto strikes, backed the steelworkers union in objecting to Nippon Steel taking over U.S. Steel, saved pensions for union workers as part of his pandemic aid and sided with unions on a new rule to make more workers eligible for overtime pay.\n\nBiden is so mindful of union workers that he recently canceled a speech planned for the conference of the National Education Association in Philadelphia after the union's staff announced a strike and formed a picket line.\n\nOne person familiar with the labor movement's thinking said there is an acknowledgment that Biden lost some ground among voters after the debate, but the unions have found that one-on-one conversations about Biden's agenda are mattering more to members and their families than his age and health. Their pitch is that Biden's agenda has directly helped union workers while Trump's plans could leave them worse off.\n\nThe AFL-CIO has raised 42 specific objections to Trump's time as president from 2017 to 2021. It noted that the Republican, who recently proposed making tips paid to workers tax-free, had also as president implemented a proposal to let bosses pocket their employees' tips, among other concerns about his tax cuts and efforts to restrict unionization.\n\nTrump has also made a play for union members, having met in January with officials in the Teamsters Union and saying afterward that although Republicans generally don't get backing from organized labor, \"in my case it's different because I've employed thousands of Teamsters and I thought we should come over and pay our respects.\"\n\nThe former president has portrayed himself as supporting blue-collar workers, with the Teamsters president Sean O'Brien later saying that there's \"no doubt\" that Trump enjoys some support from union members. O'Brien is scheduled to speak at the Republican National Convention next week in Milwaukee at Trump's invitation.\n\nIn 2020, AP VoteCast found that 16% of voters came from union households and 56% of them supported Biden. Biden and Trump essentially split non-union households in Michigan, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin, meaning that the Democrat's edge with union households was likely a critical factor in his victory.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
